At  a  Meeting  of  the  Committee  of  the  Bannatyne  Club, 
held  at  Edinburgli,  in  the  Hall  of  the  Autiquarian  Society, 
on  Saturday  the  3d  of  April,  1847  : 

Resolted, — 

That  the  Bcgistrum  Fctus  Oc  aticrbrotf)ot  as  it  exists  in  the  smaller 
volume  preserved  in  the  Library  of  the  Faculty  of  Advocates,  and  in 
the  earUer  Manuscript  lately  discovered  at  Ethie,  with  such  Cliarters, 
either  from  the  other  Records  of  the  Abbey,  or  from  oidginals  still 
extant,  as  fall  within  its  period,  be  now  printed  for  the  Membcrs 
of  the  Club,  under  the  joint  superinteudence  of  Mr.  Innes,  and  ]\Ir. 
Chalmers  of  Auldbar  ;  and  that  a  hmited  impression  upon  a  different 
paper,  not  to  exceed  One  Hundred  Copies,  be  provided  for  Subscribers, 
upon  the  terms  that  have  been  arranged  with  the  Conmiittee  of  the 
Club. 

Extracted  from  the  Minutes, 

DAVID  LAING,  Secretary.

PEEFACE. 


The  date  of  the  foimdation  of  Arbroath  is  of  some  interest  in 
Church  and  pubhc  history.  Thomas  a  Becket,  the  high  chmxh 
Archbishop,  was  slain  at  the  altar  of  his  own  church  of  Canter- 
bury,  on  the  29th  of  December  1170.  Two  years  after- 
wards,  in  1173,  he  was  canonized ;  and  within  five  years  of  his 
canonization,  and  not  more  than  seven  from  the  period  of  his 
death,  in  the  year  1178,^  WilHam  King  of  Scotland  had  founded, 
endowed,  and  dedicated  to  Saint  Thomas  the  Martyr,  the  Abbey 
of  Arbroath. 

William  was  no  admirer  of  the  Archbishop's  principles  ol 
Church  independence.  His  whole  pohcy  was  opposed  to  them. 
A  contemporary  Churchman  accuses  him  of  imitating  the  Norman 
tyranny  in   controlling   the  disposal  of  Church    preferment,"  and

he  did  not  always  testify  great  respect  for  the  Pope.  It  has  been 
suggested  that  Williani  was  personally  acquainted  with  Becket  in 
his  early  hfe,  "  when  there  was  httle  probabihty  of  his  ever  be- 
coming  a  confessor,  martyr,  and  saint."^  Was  this  the  cause,  or

Saint  Thomas  as  his  patron  saint,  and  to  entreat  his  intercession

Canterbury,  as  nothing  more  than  signs  of  the  rapid  spreading 
of  the  veneration  for  the  new  saint  of  the  high  church  party,  from 
which  his  old  opponent  himself  was  not  exempt  ?^

that  notwithstanding  monks  had  been  taken  from  Kelso  fbr  the 
house  of  Arbroath,  the  Abbot  of  Kelso  should  never  claim  any 
authority  over  the  Abbot  or  Convent  of  the  church  of  St. 
Thomas.  Wherefore,  said  they,  let  mutual  charity,  friendship 
and  prayers,  have  place  between  the  houses,  but  no  dominion 
or  power.  King  WilHam  was  present  at  that  grant,  along  with 
Mathew  Bishop  of  Aberdeen,  who  consecrated  the  Abbot,  the 
See  of  St.  Andrews  being  vacant.^  Abbot  Reginald  died  the  same 
year. 

2.  Henry,  a  monk  of  Kelso,  was  the  next  Abbot.  In  1179,  the 
same  Abbot  of  Kelso  granted  in  his  favour  a  renunciation  of  all 
authority  over  his  house  in  similar  terms  with  that  granted  to 
Abbot  Reginald.-     Hcnry  was  Abbot  till  1201,  or  later.'* 

3.  Gilbert  is  the  next  Abbot  tliat  has  occurred.  He  is  found 
in  charters  before  l^lV  and  between  1220-25.^  Gilbert  is  men- 
tioned  in  a  charter  of  Abbot  Ralph  his  successor,  as  having 
perambulated  certain  lands  of  Kenny,  in  the  shire  of  Kingoldrum.^ 

4.  Ralph  (Radulphus  de  Lamley)  was  Abbot  on  30th  March 
1226.  In  1233,  on  the  8th  of  the  Ides  of  March,  the  Abbey 
Church  was  dedicated,'^  having  been  somewhat  more  than  half 
a  century  in  building.  Abbot  Ralph  became  Bishop  of  Aberdeen 
in  1239,  when  he  probably  resigned  the  Abbacy. 

5.  Adam  was  probably  the  next  Abbot.     In  1242,  Abbot  Adam

14.  His  successor  was  Bernard  de  Linton,  who  was  Chancellor 
of  Scotland  durins  ahiiost  all  the  reign  of  Robert  the  Bruce.  He 
seems  to  have  been  appointed  to  the  Abbacy  in  1311,  when  he 
had  been  Chancellor  for  some  years.  He  continued  Abbot  and 
Chancellor  till  1328,  when,  on  the  vigil  of  St.  Phihp  and  St.  James, 
30th  April,  being  elected  to  the  See  of  Sodor,  a  pension  was 
assigned  to  him  out  of  his  Abbacy ;  and  the  Bishop  and  others 
assigning  it,  narrate  that  he  had  been  Abbot  for  seventeen  years.^ 

15.  Geoffry  immediately  succeeded  in  the  Abbacy.  He  gi'ant- 
ed  a  charter  on  March  19,  1328,"-^  and  he  was  Abbot  for  some  time 
after  the  period  at  which  the  present  volume  of  the  writs  of  the 
Abbey  closes. 

The  King,  its  founder,  was  the  great  benefactor  of  the  Abbey. 
But  it  is  astonishing  with  what  rapidity  estates  in  land,  churches 
and  tithes  were  heaped  upon  the  new  foundation,  by  the  magnates 
and  barons  of  Anffus  and  the  North.  It  is  not  uninterestino-  to 
note  the  acquisitions  of  a  single  reign. 

King  William  himself  bestowed  on  the  monks  serving  God  and 
Saint  Thomas  the  martyr  at  Aberbrothoc,  the  territory  of  Athyn  or 
Ethie,  and  Achinglas,  the  shires  of  Dunechtyn  and  Kingoldrum  ; 
a  nefs  fishing  in  Tay,  called  Stok,  and  one  in  the  North  Esk ;  a 
salt-work  in  the  Carse  of  Stirling  ;  the  ferry-boat  of  Montrose,  with 
its  land  ;  the  custody  of  "  the  Brecbennach,"  with  the  lands  of 
Forglen  attached  to  that  office  ;  a  plough  of  land  in  Monethen  or 
Mondyne  on  the  Bervy ;  a  toft  in  each  of  the  King's  burghs  and

and  the  same  family  bestowed  upon  it  lands  called  Portincraig,

XXVI  PEEFACE. 

A  person  of  the  name  of  Bricius  occurs  in  very  early  charters  as 
"judex"  of  Angus,  probably  holding  his  office  under  the  great 
Earls.  In  1219,  Adam  was  "judex"  of  the  EarFs  court.  Some 
years  later,  he  became  "judex"  of  the  King's  court,  andhis  brother 
Keraldus  succeeded  to  his  office  in  the  court  of  the  Earl,  for  in 
the  year  1227,  we  find  the  brothers  acting  together,  and  styled 
respectively  "judex"  of  Angus,  and  "judex"  of  our  Lord  the 
Kinff.  The  dwelhncp  of  Keraldus  received  the  name  of  "  Kerald- 
iston,"  now  Caraldstoun  ;  and  the  office  of  judex,  becoming  heredi- 
tary  and  taking  its  Scotcli  style  of  "  Dempster,"  gave  name  to 
the  family  who  for  many  generations  held  the  lands  of  Caraldstoun, 
and  performed  the  office  of  Dempster  of  the  Parhaments  of  Scot- 
land.  Its  functions  were  no  doubt  of  a  very  different  kind  and 
degree  from  those  fulfilled  by  the  ancient  Judex,  and  it  might  be 
interesting  to  trace,  from  these  and  other  materials,  the  progi-ess 
of  the  change. 

It  has  already  been  mentioned  tlaat  much  of  the  pedigree  of  the 
ancient  Earls  of  Angus  is  proved  from  this  Register.  It  affords 
also  valuable  information  for  the  genealogies  of  the  de  Berkeleys, 
Malherbes,  de  Rossys,  Wischards,  Middletouns,  Scots,  de  Brechins, 
Melvilles,  Arbuthnots,  Sibbalds,  Moncurs,  Mohauts,  and  other 
houses  of  Angus  and  the  Mearns,  as  well  as  of  the  Earls  of 
Buchan,  and  the  names  of  Garuiach,  le  Cheyne,  Leshe,  Feodarg, 
Mekhum,  Durward,  Walchope,  Monimusk,  and  St.  Michael,  with 
other  ancient  families  in  the  North. 

There  are  a  few  welcome  indications  of  the  domestic  manners  of 


PREFACE.  XXVII 

our  forefathers.  Thus,  a  grant  of  a  hostilage  in  Stirhng  presents  us 
with  a  fair  picture  of  a  lodging  of  the  better  sort  in  the  four- 
teenth  century — a  hall  for  meals,  with  tables  and  trestles  and 
other  furniture  ;  a  spence  with  a  buttery ;  one  or  more  chambers 
for  sleepmg ;  a  kitchen,  and  a  stable  capable  of  receiving  thirty 
horses.  Tiiey  burned  candles  of  white  tallow,  which  were  com- 
monly  called  Paris  candles.  They  used  straw  apparently  for 
beddino;,  and  tlie  hall  and  bed-cliamber  w'ere  strewed  with  rushes.' 
But  much  more  minute  and  curious  ilhistrations  of  the  manner 
of  hving  will  be  found  in  the  succeeding  part. 

Tlie  cliartulary  is  pecuharly  rich  in  notices  of  the  Culdees.  At 
Abernethv  a  convent  of  them  existed,  though,  perhaps,  in  little 
more  but  in  name,  to  the  end  of  the  reign  of  Wilham  the  Lion, 
when  they  seem  to  liave  expired,  and  there  is  no  trace  of  their 
rio-lits  or  claims  havinff  been  transferred  to  St.  Andrews.-  The 
chapter  of  Brechin  at  first  consisted  entirely  of  those  Cohuiibites. 
The  successive  Bishops  speak  of  them  with  afFection  as  "  Keledei 
nostri."  Towards  the  end  of  Wilham's  reign,  we  find  an  infusion 
of  otlier  clerks  in  the  chapter  ;  the  Prior  of  the  convent  of  Culdees 
however  being  still  the  President.^  In  1248,  the  last  year  of  the 
reign  of  Alexander  II.,  the  Culdees  have  disappeared  altogether, 
and  the  afFairs  of  the  Cathedral  are  managed  in  the  ordinary 
modern  form,  by  the  Dean  and  Chapter.''

of  Robert  the  Brucc,  with  the  parson  of  the  parish  church,  styled 
rector  of  the  church  of  St.  Braoch.' 

Tlie  church  of  Inverkeler  is  called  in  a  charter  of  King  Wil- 
ham,  the  church  of  St.  Macconoc  of  Inverkeler.  It  has  been  sug- 
gested  tliat  the  first  syllable  was  probably  a  Celtic  prefix  equivalent 
to  "saint,"  and  that  the  cliurch  was  dedicated  to  St.  Canecli  orKenny, 
the  contemporary  of  St.  Cokmiba,  who  visited  him  at  Hy,  and 
tlie  same  person  who  gives  name  to  Kilkenny.  He  is  commem- 
orated  in  the  calendar  of  the  Scotch  Church  on  the  llth  of  October. 

When  we  consider  the  long  and  united  efforts  required  in  the 
early  state  of  the  arts  for  throwing  a  bridge  over  any  considerable 
river,  the  early  occurrence  of  bridges  may  be  well  admitted  as  one 
of  the  best  tests  of  civiHzation  and  national  prosperity.  The  bridge 
over  the  North  water  has  ah-eady  been  mentioned.  We  find  a 
brido;e  existino-  over  the  Esk  at  Brechin,  and  the  land  of  Drumsleid 
appropriated  for  its  support,  in  the  early  part  of  the  13th  century." 
In  that  age,  we  had  a  bridge  over  the  Tay  at  Perth ;  bridges  over 
the  Esks  at  Brechin  and  Marykirk ;  a  bridge  over  the  Dee  at 
Kincardine  0'Neill,  probably  another  at  Durris,  one  near  Aber- 
deen,  and  one  at  the  mouth  of  Glenmuick  :  even  a  bridge  over

when  she  ceased  to  be  a  separate  kingdom  in  1707.

of  Statuta  Concillii  Scoticani,  wliich,  imperfect  as  they  are,

Considering  the  Registrum  Vetus  as  a  record  of  the  age  of 
Abbot  Bernard,  that  period  has  been  adopted  as  the  Hmit  of  the 
present  vohime,  which  will  be  found  to  embrace  all  the  ancient 
matter  of  the  Registrum  Vetus,  as  well  as  all  the  documents  con- 
tained  in  the  other  Registers  mentioned,  that  are  of  dates  earher 
than  the  end  of  tlie  reign  of  Robert  I. 

In  arranging  these  materials  for  the  press,  it  has  been  thought  okdekofPri 
proper  to  preserve  the  integrity,  and  even  the  shape  and  order  of 
the  ancient  Abbey  Register,  as  it  is  found  imperfectly  in  the  Ethie 
MS.,  and  more  complete  in  the  "  Registrum  Vetus."     The  body 
of  that   Register  is  divided  into  several  heads,  according  to  tlie

embraced  bv  this  vohmie,  as  have  come  to  the  knowledge  of  the 
Editors.  It  is  not  to  be  doubted  that  many  more  of  equal  or 
greater  interest  remain  unknown  and  neglected  in  the  charter- 
rooms  of  Angus  and  the  Nortli  ;  and  one  advantage  of  the  second 
part  of  the  Register  being  for  some  time  delayed  is,  that  further 
opportunities  may  be  afForded  for  bringing  these  to  hght  for 
illustrating  the  second  volume. 

The  present  vohime  will  be  regarded  with  great  interest  bv 
the  historian  as  well  as  the  local  antiquary.  It  points  at  tlie 
first  settlement  of  many  districts,  and  the  earhest  traces  of  civih- 
zation  ;  some  very  interesting  particulars  of  Church  antiquities, 
and  tiic  various  races  from  which  our  population  draws  its 
origin.  It  ilhistrates  the  descent  and  transmission  of  lands  widelv 
scattered  over  thrce  counties,  and  the  early  liistory  of  some 
of  the  greatest  and  most  interesting  Scotch  famiHes.     The  suc-

ceeding  one,  whilst  it  traces  dowi   the  descent  ot"  property,  will

manners,  and    the  whole    social   condition  of  the  people  of  the

The  buildings  of  the  Abbey  of  Arbroath,  begun  in  1 178,  brought 

near  to   their   completion  at  the   time   of  the   dedication   of  its 

cluu-ch  in    1233,    tluough    the   decay   of   so    many   centuries,  in 

spite  of  violence  and  long  neglect,  and  barbarous  modern  repairs, 

still  afford  a  few  specimens   of  good   Norman  architecture,  and 

parts  of  several  later  styles.     In   the  middle  of  last  century,  Ur. 

Johnson  said  "  he  should  scarcely  have  regi'etted  his  journey  "  to 

Scotland,  "  had  it  afforded  nothing  more  than  the  siglit  of  Aber- 

brothick."     The   taste  for  church   architecture  had  not  then   re- 

vived  among   us  ;  aiid  Johnson's  was  only  an  impression   which 

would  have   been   produced    by   the   associations  of  any  ancient 

ecclesiastical   structure.     The  present   state  of  the  ruins,  and  of 

the  few  remaining  nu)numents  of  Arbroath,  some  plates  of  wliicli 

accompany  the  present  vohune,  will  be  more  particularly  ilhistrated 

in  the  following  one.
